  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ac00db77-a47c-4fe6-aa41-e8c39f163b95'

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/391709-i-have-a-question-regarding-a-flashback">I have a question regarding a flashback scene included in this book. Holmes climbs in Mary&#39;s window after realizing he has hurt her with the response he gave her to a question she asked. As Holmes exits her room, she asks him not to come to her house again as long as her aunt lives there. She stumbles over her words and Holmes looks at her gently. What she&#39;s trying to say. Can anyone help me?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_391709'><a id="like_count_community_question_391709" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/391709?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F93938.A_Letter_of_Mary">like</a></span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/2174893-lenore"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/2174893-lenore">Lenore</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think she is trying to say that she does not want to associate in her mind Holmes with this house. She sees Holmes as a refuge from this house, as s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think she is trying to say that she does not want to associate in her mind Holmes with this house. She sees Holmes as a refuge from this house, as she currently sees the house as a kind of prison. No longer the case once she has ousted her aunt and removed all traces of her.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_326970565" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326970565" />
    <a title="Natalie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4936267-natalie"><img alt="Natalie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367812911p2/4936267.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/326970565?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie" href="/user/show/4936267-natalie">Natalie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4936267-natalie?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326970565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2918692585891396915">Guilty. I committed a great reading faux-pas. <br/><br/>Years ago I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91661.The_Beekeeper_s_Apprentice__Mary_Russell___1_' title='The Beekeeper's Apprentice (Mary Russell, #1) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Beekeeper's Apprentice</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104737.A_Monstrous_Regiment_of_Women__Mary_Russell___2_' title='A Monstrous Regiment of Women (Mary Russell, #2) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</a>. My book group was reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91661.The_Beekeeper_s_Apprentice__Mary_Russell___1_' title='The Beekeeper's Apprentice (Mary Russell, #1) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Beekeeper's Apprentice</a> , so I reread it and loved it even more than I remembered. I decided to buy the next two books in the series, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104737.A_Monstrous_Regiment_of_Women__Mary_Russell___2_' title='A Monstrous Regiment of Women (Mary Russell, #2) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93938.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_' title='A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Letter of Mary</a>, but alas, book #3, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93938.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_' title='A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Letter of Mary</a>, arrived first. <br/><br/>This was quite frustrating. I was so excited to read them and now I was presented with a dilemma. On the one</span>
  <span id="freeText2918692585891396915" style="display:none">Guilty. I committed a great reading faux-pas. <br /><br />Years ago I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91661.The_Beekeeper_s_Apprentice__Mary_Russell___1_" title="The Beekeeper's Apprentice (Mary Russell, #1) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Beekeeper's Apprentice</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104737.A_Monstrous_Regiment_of_Women__Mary_Russell___2_" title="A Monstrous Regiment of Women (Mary Russell, #2) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">A Monstrous Regiment of Women</a>. My book group was reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91661.The_Beekeeper_s_Apprentice__Mary_Russell___1_" title="The Beekeeper's Apprentice (Mary Russell, #1) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Beekeeper's Apprentice</a> , so I reread it and loved it even more than I remembered. I decided to buy the next two books in the series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104737.A_Monstrous_Regiment_of_Women__Mary_Russell___2_" title="A Monstrous Regiment of Women (Mary Russell, #2) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">A Monstrous Regiment of Women</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93938.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_" title="A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">A Letter of Mary</a>, but alas, book #3, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93938.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_" title="A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">A Letter of Mary</a>, arrived first. <br /><br />This was quite frustrating. I was so excited to read them and now I was presented with a dilemma. On the one hand, I believe that books should be read in order, it’s really the only way. On the other hand, I was aching to read more in this series. I decided to satiate my desire by just reading a few pages....<br /><br />Of course I should have known that I’m not to be trusted to stop reading a good book. And it was so good, so tantalizing, I found myself reading clear through to the end. I justified it by saying that I had already read A Monstrous Regiment of Women at one point, but I still felt like I’d been caught with my hand in the cookie jar.<br /><br />There is something so perfect about these books. I enjoy the mystery but I love the dialogue. I love the setting and the characters. I love the realism and how the characters pop to life. <br />Mary and Sherlock are delightful in every way. Surprisingly, the age difference doesn’t bother me. It’s because their relationship is so sweet and tender. Most romances in books these days seem to play the lust card too heavily, focusing on the physical instead of the emotional and mental. King hardly ever touches the physical side of the relationship between Sherlock and Mary, but how much they care is felt in every word and gesture. That is a TRUE love story. It also makes you really think about what is important in relationships. <br /><br />After I read this book I did the only thing a true book lover could do when presented with a fabulous series – I got on Amazon and bought all the rest. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2918692585891396915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2944013871" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2944013871" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2944013871?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=summer-of-sherlock">summer-of-sherlock</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=mysteries-thrillers">mysteries-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=sherlock-holmes">sherlock-holmes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2944013871" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16667426738069691817"><b>
  <i>***2019 The Summer of Sherlock***</i>
</b><br/><br/>Whatever I may think of the whole Sherlock Holmes/Mary Russell relationship hook that King has used as the basis for this series, she is a masterful writer of the mystery genre in my opinion. I am perhaps biased, as I thoroughly enjoyed my time as a university student and studied three courses in Ancient Greek as well as a fair bit of Classical history (mostly influenced by H. Rider Haggard’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/682681.She_A_History_of_Adventure__She___1_' title='She A History of Adventure (She, #1) by H. Rider Haggard' rel='nofollow noopener'>She: A History of Adventure</a>, I confess) and during that period I wa</span>
  <span id="freeText16667426738069691817" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>***2019 The Summer of Sherlock***</i>
</b><br /><br />Whatever I may think of the whole Sherlock Holmes/Mary Russell relationship hook that King has used as the basis for this series, she is a masterful writer of the mystery genre in my opinion. I am perhaps biased, as I thoroughly enjoyed my time as a university student and studied three courses in Ancient Greek as well as a fair bit of Classical history (mostly influenced by H. Rider Haggard’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/682681.She_A_History_of_Adventure__She___1_" title="She A History of Adventure (She, #1) by H. Rider Haggard" rel="nofollow noopener">She: A History of Adventure</a>, I confess) and during that period I was severely tempted by Biblical historicity and documents such as the Dead Sea Scrolls--I can envision myself being just as immersed in theological studies as Mary Russell. <br /><br />Since reading the first two Theodora Goss novels (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34728925.The_Strange_Case_of_the_Alchemist_s_Daughter" title="The Strange Case of the Alchemist's Daughter by Theodora Goss" rel="nofollow noopener">The Strange Case of the Alchemist's Daughter</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36373571.European_Travel_for_the_Monstrous_Gentlewoman" title="European Travel for the Monstrous Gentlewoman by Theodora Goss" rel="nofollow noopener">European Travel for the Monstrous Gentlewoman</a>), I am also developing an affection for cross-pollination between fictional works and King provides that in several examples. I was amused when Mary returned from a research day in Oxford, mentioning that she had met a man named Tolkien who was involved in runes, mythology and linguistics. <br /><br />But the pièce de résistance was an encounter by Mary during an “undercover” adventure with Lord Peter Wimsey! He is never directly identified, but she does call him Pete and his behaviour is unmistakable. She has just been diverted from the buffet by the presence of two women who will unwittingly unmask her and has wandered back to a music room to contemplate her options. There she discovers Wimsey improvising “a three-way hybrid of Schubert’s “March Militaire” performed as a Goldberg Variation by Bach with Scott Joplin occasionally elbowing in,” based on Yes, We Have No Bananas. Mary enjoys watching him, “witnessing one of nature’s rare creatures in its own habitat.”<br /><br /><blockquote>He screwed his monocle into place with a gesture of buckling on armour, then glided smoothly out into the crowd. I watched with amusement as he greeted his hostess, kissed the fingers of a matched brace of dowagers, shook various hands, greeted the colonel and said something that made him laugh, scooped up three glasses of champagne from a passing tray, and finally, with the ease of a champion sheepdog, cut out his two victims from the flock. Within four minutes from leaving my side, he was strolling down the terrace stones, one fluttering female on each arm, and I stepped out to take a plate. Rule, Britannia, with an aristocracy like that.</blockquote><br /><br />King, to my way of thinking, has perfectly nailed the spirit of Dorothy Sayers in this vignette. It made me ridiculously happy. It’s the sheepdog reference that cinched it for me--that is Sayers to the nth degree.<br /><br />My Summer of Sherlock is drawing to a close, but I am sure that I will continue reading this series for some time to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16667426738069691817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_963645095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/963645095" />
    <a title="Stephanie Swint" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8720943-stephanie-swint"><img alt="Stephanie Swint" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370546825p2/8720943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/963645095?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Swint" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Swint" href="/user/show/8720943-stephanie-swint">Stephanie Swint</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer963645095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18193387840302923087">When I finished King’s second novel in this series, ‘A Monstrous Regiment of Women,’ I was left a bit uncomfortable and unhappy. King had always kept Mary and Sherlock’s relationship as a mentor and one of a guardian. At the end of that book Sherlock proposes. Their relationship while strained and questioning in the book hadn’t been romantic, but Mary had turned 21 and their relationship caused questions, especially when traveling together.<br/><br/>My first response was that King was throwing in a needle</span>
  <span id="freeText18193387840302923087" style="display:none">When I finished King’s second novel in this series, ‘A Monstrous Regiment of Women,’ I was left a bit uncomfortable and unhappy. King had always kept Mary and Sherlock’s relationship as a mentor and one of a guardian. At the end of that book Sherlock proposes. Their relationship while strained and questioning in the book hadn’t been romantic, but Mary had turned 21 and their relationship caused questions, especially when traveling together.<br /><br />My first response was that King was throwing in a needless romance and I was unhappy. Reading this series for me, however, is a family affair. My ever practical and precocious niece was put in the position where she had to educate her aunt(kindly of course.) She reminded me that I was judging the time of the book by present day values. I needed to remember that Sherlock and Mary could not continue working together without causing scandal and destroying Mary’s reputation. A marriage between a twenty-one year old woman and a fifty year old confirmed bachelor out of mutual respect rather than love was far more respectable than working alone together unchaperoned. My niece, of course, was absolutely correct. This, then thirteen year old is extremely smart, and her valuable insight allowed me to once again respect King’s skill and intelligence as a writer.<br /><br />This settled, I picked up ‘A Letter to Marry,’ with an audible sigh of contentment. This book starts with a visit from an elderly scholar and friend they had met in Palestine in book one. She gave a generous gift of a papyrus believed to have genuinely been written by Mary Magdalene. You can imagine how this would enrage and turn the academic community upside down if validated. Shortly, after their friend departs company she was in an automobile accident and dies. Foul play can not be ruled out, and Sherlock weedles Mary away from her research to investigate the cause of death.<br /><br />King did a wonderful job. I read mysteries frequently to escape. I’m not generally looking for an overly intelligent masterpiece but King is an exception to that rule. She provides an intelligent and exciting read. She creates a mystery I rarely guess the outcome of and a fabulously accurate piece of historical fiction as well. If you like Sherlock Holmes pick this up; if you enjoy pre WW II historical fiction pick this up; if you enjoy a fictionalized study of sociological gender roles and the human mind pick this up. I’m sounding like a broken record. I recommend you read it, but remember the series starts with ‘The Beekeepers Apprentice.’</span>
  <a data-text-id="18193387840302923087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13614926-caidyn-he-him-his?shelf=sherlock">sherlock</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer12064850257357324864">Another lovely book in this series.<br/><br/>I don't have much to say about it, honestly. I thought the mystery was pretty good, although it didn't fully consume me as I would have liked. I still love Holmes and Mary. They're so cute together especially since (spoiler for the second book, so don't read the rest of this sentence) they're married now. That comment about the firstborn, though.... baby on the way? I can't see Holmes having a baby -- although it was hinted that he already had an illegitimate s</span>
  <span id="freeText12064850257357324864" style="display:none">Another lovely book in this series.<br /><br />I don't have much to say about it, honestly. I thought the mystery was pretty good, although it didn't fully consume me as I would have liked. I still love Holmes and Mary. They're so cute together especially since (spoiler for the second book, so don't read the rest of this sentence) they're married now. That comment about the firstborn, though.... baby on the way? I can't see Holmes having a baby -- although it was hinted that he already had an illegitimate son, but never was talked about again -- at his age, and Mary doesn't seem the mothering type, but who knows! This series has a lot of books. One thing I love about Ms King's portrayal is that she doesn't try to make them something they're not. Holmes is an ass half the time, yet he has a sweet side that comes out every so often. Same with Mary; she might be brilliant, but she's still human. They're all human characters, even the side ones. And, to me, that's a great skill since Holmes is such an enigma.<br /><br />Another key point in this book was the suffragette movement. I'm more familiar with the American one than the British one, so I'm not sure much on the facts. The second book in the series also deals with that, but more in depth than this one. It's not surprising that Ms King used it given the time period this is written in, but it felt a touch overused for two books in a row.<br /><br />Just enjoyable. Four loved-every-moment stars.</span>

  <span id="freeText7726923014281299554" style="display:none">Mary Russell has noted the tell-tale signs of boredom in her husband, Sherlock Holmes, when his mind is not being stimulated with a case. His malaise ends when the couple receives an unexpected visit from a former acquaintance, Dorothy Ruskin, an amateur archeologist. Ms. Ruskin is seeking Mary's assistance in authenticating and translating a 1st century papyrus letter reportedly written by Mary Magdalene. <div id="review_1521175973" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1521175973" />
    <a title="Mike Finn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5248388-mike-finn"><img alt="Mike Finn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487163372p2/5248388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1521175973?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Finn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Finn" href="/user/show/5248388-mike-finn">Mike Finn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781427245038" href="/book/show/30827586-a-letter-of-mary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5248388-mike-finn?shelf=audio-book">audio-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5248388-mike-finn?shelf=sherlock-holmes">sherlock-holmes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1521175973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16715966564599299726"><b>Melancholic but strangely compelling</b><br/><br/>"A Letter Of Mary" is the third book in the Mary Russell series and the first one in which we see how Mary Russell and Sherlock Holmes function as a married couple.<br/><br/>They are both formidable people: Holmes, in his sixties, almost retired from a career he made largely in the previous century, but still with a restless hunger for the intriguing; Russell, an independent, wealthy, woman in her twenties, with a passion for scholarship and a tendency to lose herself i</span>
  <span id="freeText16715966564599299726" style="display:none"><b>Melancholic but strangely compelling</b><br /><br />"A Letter Of Mary" is the third book in the Mary Russell series and the first one in which we see how Mary Russell and Sherlock Holmes function as a married couple.<br /><br />They are both formidable people: Holmes, in his sixties, almost retired from a career he made largely in the previous century, but still with a restless hunger for the intriguing; Russell, an independent, wealthy, woman in her twenties, with a passion for scholarship and a tendency to lose herself in her books for days at a time. The two of them with an almost decade-long relationship that has moved from master and apprentice, through professional partnership and on to a marriage that is, to both of them, the defining aspect of their lives and yet the part of it that have the least knowledge and experience of.<br /><br />They are serious, capable, responsible people. They can be intimidating, a fact they often take advantage of. They practice deception ruthlessly in the pursuit of their prey. Yet, together, they can laugh at their own hauteur and theatrics and they know each other so well that deception between them is impossible.<br /><br />In "A Letter Of Mary" Russell and Holmes work to find the murderer of a friend, yet they spend most of their time apart, pursuing different prey and restricted in contacting each other because of the personae they have adopted for the hunt.<br /><br />I found the overall tone of the book to be quite melancholic. The sources of the melancholy speak to the quality of the story-telling and character-building. The killing of Russell's friend carries a real weight of loss at possibilities ended and produces an indignant anger, that sometimes flairs into wrath, at those who would take a life. This something that is often missing from detectives stories where the dead are just body-shaped puzzles to be solved. The second source of melancholy comes from the attacks on Russell and Holmes' way of living: their home is violated, they are separated for the first time in their marriage, they are forced to confront the new fear they now have of losing each other.<br /><br />Russel and Holmes push through this melancholy with intellect and courage, powered by a strong sense of obligation to the dead woman and a refusal to give in to those who act against them. They are a source of strength to each other and their love is as deeply serious as everything else in their lives. I found it refreshing to see a commitment based on knowledge of the other person rather than a fog of romantic imaginings and hopes.<br /><br />The plot revolves around the death of an older woman, a Gertrude Bell sort of woman, who runs archaeological digs in the Middle East and occasionally acts in the service of the Crown.<br /><br />There are multiple possible murderers to be hunted. Russell's hunt requires her to take on the persona of Mary Small and work as a secretary for a Colonel well known for his temper and his misogyny. Although this experience appears to require Russell to become someone else, the process and her reactions to the men she meets causes her to learn a great deal more about herself.<br /><br />Much of the action in this book takes place off stage. The characters spend a great deal of time sitting together, sharing information and debating conclusions. The fights and threats of the two previous books are absent. I found this change of pace both refreshing and credible.<br /><br />I believe that, with this book, the series crossed over the threshold where I will be less interested in the plots of the individual books and more concerned with the development of the relationship between Russell and Holmes and the insights they have into life and how it should be lived along the way.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16715966564599299726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_199000850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/199000850" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/199000850?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer199000850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer243822700004279997"><br/>This is the third book in King's Mary Russell / Sherlock Holmes series, and the more I read, the more I like the series. Russell is intelligent, lively, interesting and feisty - a worthy match for Holmes. King has said that Russell is what Holmes would be if he were female, born in the 20th Century and interested in theology (or words to that effect). In my view, that aspect of Russell has become clearer as the series has progressed. It is also what makes the pairing of the very young Russell an</span>
  <span id="freeText243822700004279997" style="display:none"><br />This is the third book in King's Mary Russell / Sherlock Holmes series, and the more I read, the more I like the series. Russell is intelligent, lively, interesting and feisty - a worthy match for Holmes. King has said that Russell is what Holmes would be if he were female, born in the 20th Century and interested in theology (or words to that effect). In my view, that aspect of Russell has become clearer as the series has progressed. It is also what makes the pairing of the very young Russell and the aging Holmes both understandable and acceptable. Russell, a woman born in 1900, is representative of a new era. Her age and her gender are central to her importance as a character, and are just as important as the fact that her intellect is equal to that of Holmes. <br /><br />If anything lets this novel down, it's the resolution of the mystery. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ A resolution which depends on the victim taking elaborate precautions to write and hide a new will because she "felt" that she was in danger felt a little contrived. And it may just be that I finished reading the book when I was tired, but it was not completely clear to me that discovering the secret of the box and the will actually helped nail the villains. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But I quibble. In reality, what makes this book and the series as a whole worth reading is not the mystery, but the characters and the setting, as well as the atmosphere they help create. <br /><br />For me, the highlight of the novel was the cameo appearance of Peter Wimsey in Chapter 17. There he was, Lord Peter to the life! It made me wonder whether the Sayers estate would have done better to get King rather than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8733.Jill_Paton_Walsh" title="Jill Paton Walsh" rel="nofollow noopener">Jill Paton Walsh</a> to complete <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/132671.Thrones__Dominations" title="Thrones, Dominations by Dorothy L. Sayers" rel="nofollow noopener">Thrones, Dominations</a>. There's also a mention of Tolkien in the novel, just to add a bit of Oxford literary flavour. <br /><br />Overall, this was a good read. The next book in the series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/184059.The_Moor__Mary_Russell___4_" title="The Moor (Mary Russell, #4) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moor</a>, is waiting in the pile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="243822700004279997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2317864897?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue Smith" href="/user/show/3583076-sue-smith">Sue Smith</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427382" href="/book/show/366164.A_Letter_of_Mary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3583076-sue-smith?shelf=books-read-in-2018">books-read-in-2018</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2317864897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15255933205544027146">I love Laurie King's writing, she's masterful at sweeping you up into the story without getting you bogged down in details. And it's ever so clever how she has insinuated Sherlock Holmes into the mix. Who would have though about him marrying?! Great way to keep him in the game without letting him take over the entire mystery, all the while keeping their relationship on an equal footing. Very lovely writing indeed!<br/><br/>This one starts with an unexpected visit from an old scholastic friend of Mary Russ</span>
  <span id="freeText15255933205544027146" style="display:none">I love Laurie King's writing, she's masterful at sweeping you up into the story without getting you bogged down in details. And it's ever so clever how she has insinuated Sherlock Holmes into the mix. Who would have though about him marrying?! Great way to keep him in the game without letting him take over the entire mystery, all the while keeping their relationship on an equal footing. Very lovely writing indeed!<br /><br />This one starts with an unexpected visit from an old scholastic friend of Mary Russell who spends her time in the middle East doing anthropological digs, but mainly focusing on the aspect of the role of the woman in those ancient cultures. Not that you get to far into that, but she brings up a very intriguing piece for the examination and opinion of her one and only female scholar who also has an interest in the subject of the ancient woman and her place in society, our own Mary Russell. It's left in her hands and the next thing you know her friend is run down by an automobile and our married crime solving duo is on the trail to determine if it's an accident or murder.<br /><br />It's murder of course! So the story whips into the who dunnit and the sleuthing to catch said criminal. It's not your typical mystery - you get a bit more of the humdrum of the sleuthing, the tediousness and the marginalizing of the importance of one characters investigation over the other. All the while exposing the impossibleness, the marginalizing of a woman's voice and how it's prevalent in everyday society - how men fight to keep it that way. Some things are hard to change.<br /><br />Great story! Looking forward to carrying on with the series!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15255933205544027146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5532746103371144593" style="display:none">Am very much enjoying this!<br /><br />I think one of the tings I like about this series is that there is considerable intellect involved, both in the characters of Russell and Holmes themselves, and in the manner in which the mystery is solved.<br /><br />In this 3rd installment we see the continuing development of Russell as Holmes' assistant, partner, and wife, and the evolution of the relationship between the two of them.The mysterious Mary M. letter, and it's implications, reflects the character of Russell, her relationship with Holmes.<br />I am as intrigued with the people in this series as I am with the mysteries, and am anxious to start the next book.<br /><br />In addition to the above, I like King's style of writing. I don't feel as though sentences and thoughts have been shortened or hacked away at in an effort to make reading "easier" , as often seems the case with many authors these days. </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/742214182" />

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=mystery-british">mystery-british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=mystery-historical">mystery-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=detective">detective</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer742214182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2773825226149263593"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12383496.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_' title='A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Letter of Mary</a> is the 3rd book in the Mary Russell / Sherlock Holmes mystery series by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6760.Laurie_R__King' title='Laurie R. King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Laurie R. King</a>. I have to say that I continue to have mixed feelings about the series. The concept in itself, Russell who started off being mentored by Holmes is now his wife and works cases with him. Holmes, himself, is basically retired from detective work and they live on a piec of land in Sussex where Holmes raises bees and Russell works on a degree at Oxford while also spending time with her husband.<br/>An </span>
  <span id="freeText2773825226149263593" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12383496.A_Letter_of_Mary__Mary_Russell___3_" title="A Letter of Mary (Mary Russell, #3) by Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">A Letter of Mary</a> is the 3rd book in the Mary Russell / Sherlock Holmes mystery series by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6760.Laurie_R__King" title="Laurie R. King" rel="nofollow noopener">Laurie R. King</a>. I have to say that I continue to have mixed feelings about the series. The concept in itself, Russell who started off being mentored by Holmes is now his wife and works cases with him. Holmes, himself, is basically retired from detective work and they live on a piec of land in Sussex where Holmes raises bees and Russell works on a degree at Oxford while also spending time with her husband.<br />An acquaintance of Russell, a lady archaeologist, Dorothy Ruskin, visits them, gives them a letter she had found in a dig in the Middle East, a letter purported to have been written by Mary Magdalene to her sister. The next day, Ruskin is found dead, hit by a car in London. Holmes and Russell get involved, suspect that Ruskin has been murdered for some reason. The same day, Holmes' home is broken into and searched. Further suspicion now that it was murder and not an accident.<br />The rest of the story involves their investigation, with help by the son of Lestrade and also Sherlock's brother Mycroft. Both Holmes and Mary follow different suspects; one a misogynist Colonel, the other the sister of Dorothy. <br />So that's the barest outline. The story is interesting, but, personally, I do find it difficult to warm to Russell. At one point she calls Holmes a prig and I thought, no, you're the prig. She is a strong-willed, intelligent character, but so much time is spent with her kind of anti-snobbishness that she seems to be a snob. I'm not saying this correctly. I like Holmes, I like Mycroft, I like Lestrade and Russell is OK in her own way. I liked the story, but I didn't love it. At the end, I thought, OK, there you go. So what. Does that mean I won't read any of the others I still have on my bookshelf. No, it doesn't, but I'll read a few other books before I do. Try the series and let me know what you think. (3 stars) </span>
  <a data-text-id="2773825226149263593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_743577662" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/743577662" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1256262-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442983573p2/1256262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/743577662?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/1256262-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256262-ron?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer743577662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12547662640330454295">This is a good book and a well-written addition to King's Mary Russell series, but I was thirty pages into it when I realized it was another <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code__Robert_Langdon___2_' title='The Da Vinci Code (Robert Langdon, #2) by Dan Brown' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Da Vinci Code</a> ripoffs. Groan. Can't these people come up with their own all-you-thought-you-knew-about-Christianity-is-wrong plots? This one is actually more realistic than Dan Brown's, but it's been done. <br/><br/>It would probably amaze these people to know that their "abject terror" of the controversy is a bit overblown. Of course Jesus had female followers. </span>
  <span id="freeText12547662640330454295" style="display:none">This is a good book and a well-written addition to King's Mary Russell series, but I was thirty pages into it when I realized it was another <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code__Robert_Langdon___2_" title="The Da Vinci Code (Robert Langdon, #2) by Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">The Da Vinci Code</a> ripoffs. Groan. Can't these people come up with their own all-you-thought-you-knew-about-Christianity-is-wrong plots? This one is actually more realistic than Dan Brown's, but it's been done. <br /><br />It would probably amaze these people to know that their "abject terror" of the controversy is a bit overblown. Of course Jesus had female followers. Of course some of them were very influential in the early church. Of course not everyone today agrees with the role assigned to those women by church leaders since. And . . . ? What the big controversy?<br /><br />This supposed Mary letter is only the McGuffin: it triggers the action, but is not incident to the action.<br /><br />King drops many Easter Eggs, such as Mary Russell's visit to the Eagle and Child pub in Oxford: future host to the famous Inkling literary circle. Likewise, graphology is presented as a budding science, as it was then thought to be. Still, 2 or 200 nineteenth century writing samples of Greek and Hebrew would reveal little about a first century writer.<br /><br />Still, a good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12547662640330454295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review743577662">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer2147310022142814502">Excellent addition to Laurie R.   <span id="freeText2147310022142814502" style="display:none">Excellent addition to Laurie R. <div id="review_214839972" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/214839972" />
    <a title="Kam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5399361-kam"><img alt="Kam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466042158p2/5399361.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/214839972?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kam" href="/user/show/5399361-kam">Kam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427382" href="/book/show/366164.A_Letter_of_Mary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5399361-kam?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5399361-kam?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer214839972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14650868251194282076">After the disappointment of <i>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</i>, I was not very much inclined to read the next installment in the Mary Russell series, titled <i>A Letter of Mary</i>. I decided to forge on, though, in the hopes that this will prove better than the last one, and that it might wash away some of the bitter aftertaste of disappointment in the last book.<br/><br/>In this novel, Holmes and Russell appear to be settling well into married life after the events of <i>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</i>, when they are vi</span>
  <span id="freeText14650868251194282076" style="display:none">After the disappointment of <i>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</i>, I was not very much inclined to read the next installment in the Mary Russell series, titled <i>A Letter of Mary</i>. I decided to forge on, though, in the hopes that this will prove better than the last one, and that it might wash away some of the bitter aftertaste of disappointment in the last book.<br /><br />In this novel, Holmes and Russell appear to be settling well into married life after the events of <i>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</i>, when they are visited in their (formerly just Holmes's) home in Sussex by an old friend: archaeologist Dorothy Ruskin, whom Holmes and Russell met on their travels through Palestine in the first book, <i>The Beekeeper's Apprentice</i>. The visit deepens Russell's appreciation for Ruskin, and the latter gives into Russell's keeping a little box she claims to have acquired from an Arab in Palestine. Inside the box is a scroll of papyrus which, when Russell reads it, appears to be a letter written by one Mariam (or Mary) to her sister in Magdala. It does not take much to put two and two together, even for the reader: the "Mariam," or "Mary" of the letter, could only be Mary of Magdala - Mary Magdalene. In the letter, Mariam claims that she is a follower - a disciple - of a man named Joshua, whom she calls the "Anointed One." Joshua, when traced forwards through time, is actually a variation of the name "Jesus."<br /><br />A Mariam, or Mary, of Magdala who is a disciple if an Anointed One named Joshua, or Jesus. Anyone who has had even the slightest bit of contact with <i>The Da Vinci Code</i> and the controversies associated with it will know precisely what is being referred to here: the position of Mary Magdalene within the circle of followers, or disciples, who gathered around the man who would later be called Jesus Christ. <br /><br />The letter proves to be an interesting puzzle for Russell, whose analysis of the letter is interspersed throughout the rest of the story. While there is no mention made (thankfully) of the idea that Mary Magdalene could have been Jesus's wife, Russell's thoughts on what this letter could mean if unleashed upon London in the early 1920s are fascinating. The suffragette movement and the ideals it fought for, which were grounded in the events of World War I and which gained steam in the years immediately after, were always present, even in the first novel. While they were not foregrounded in <i>The Beekeeper's Apprentice</i>, they were given much more attention in <i>A Monstrous Regiment of Women</i>, and play an important role once again in <i>A Letter of Mary</i>. Russell's thoughts on the letter sharpen when she is forced to go undercover in the household of a particularly misogynistic colonel, and it is while she is playing secretary to said colonel that her musings on the role of women and their continued participation in the future become clearest and most interesting.<br /><br />This book was better than the last one, which is why it receives three stars as opposed to the two I gave the last one. But it gets nothing higher than that because of one particular annoying feature: a red herring. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Throughout the novel I had been led to believe that the letter Russell kept in her possession was far more important than it turned out to be. Indeed, the letter was nothing more than a red herring at most, and a plot device at the least: a means to get Russell and Holmes involved in the case later on. While I had no trouble with the plot, it was the motives of the perpetrators, as they were revealed in the last part of the book, that annoyed me the most. Given the importance placed on the letter - it was a crucial part of the plot for a good three-fourths of the novel - I was disappointed to figure out at the end that it had nothing to do with the crime at all. The letter was nothing more than a gift, and the motives of the perpetrators were driven more by greed than any desire to keep the information in the papyrus a secret. Indeed, the mastermind had no knowledge, or even interest, in the letter Russell was keeping, and really couldn't have cared less about whether or not its contents were divulged to the general public. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Despite that problem, however, I do find this book slightly stronger than the last one, and I hope it is the sign of better things to come in this series. Russell's voice is, as always, entertaining and a joy to read, especially when she starts snarking back at her husband - but then, Holmes always gives as good as he gets, and there's nothing wrong with that. Some readers might blush like Mycroft when they figure out the other aspects of Holmes and Russell's marriage, but it does strengthen King's stand that her Holmes is more human than Doyle's. <br /><br />One other thing that might be of interest to those who love fantasy literature as well as mysteries: a particularly famous and deeply revered fantasist out of Oxford is briefly mentioned by Russell in the course of the story. Finding his name tickled me to no end, as I am a fan of said fantasist, and I hope that others take the same pleasure in it that did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14650868251194282076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42065406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42065406" />
    <a title="Trin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113980-trin"><img alt="Trin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181029461p2/113980.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42065406?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trin" href="/user/show/113980-trin">Trin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=historicalfiction">historicalfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=ficbutnot">ficbutnot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer42065406&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42065406" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11682386056624099733">A mystery made up almost entirely of red herrings. Holmes and Russell continue to be charming, but I was frustrated by how much of the narrative turned out to be irrelevant. Even the letter of the title—supposedly a piece of correspondence between Mary Magdalene and her sister, which is, you know, generally the type of thing da Vinci writes codes about and plots are based around—is, as far as this story and even its characters are concerned, essentially meaningless. If Holmes can solve the entir</span>
  <span id="freeText11682386056624099733" style="display:none">A mystery made up almost entirely of red herrings. Holmes and Russell continue to be charming, but I was frustrated by how much of the narrative turned out to be irrelevant. Even the letter of the title—supposedly a piece of correspondence between Mary Magdalene and her sister, which is, you know, generally the type of thing da Vinci writes codes about and plots are based around—is, as far as this story and even its characters are concerned, essentially meaningless. If Holmes can solve the entire mystery by spending a couple days putting up some wallpaper, then why do we have to spend ages hanging out with Russell while she pretends to be some rich dick’s secretary? None of the mucking about was even particularly illuminating in regards to her character or her relationship with Holmes.<br /><br />I was, however, amused by Lord Peter Wimsey’s little cameo, especially since not long before he arrived, I’d been thinking that we’d entered the time period when he’d be back from the war and running about solving crimes, too. Handwaving the fact that in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2238.Isabel_Allende" title="Isabel Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">Sayers</a>’ books, Sherlock Holmes is frequently referred to as fictional, making a crossover technically impossible for sticklers like myself, I will allow myself to titter and enjoy the occasional pleasures inherent in wacky published fanfic such as this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11682386056624099733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1345943631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1345943631" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1343864-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597876967p2/1343864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1345943631?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/1343864-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427382" href="/book/show/366164.A_Letter_of_Mary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=cbr-book-exchange">cbr-book-exchange</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=anglophilia">anglophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=orphalins">orphalins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=class-gender-race">class-gender-race</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=mysteries">mysteries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=shop-on-your-own-shelves-2018">shop-on-your-own-shelves-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=murder-most-foul">murder-most-foul</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=sherlockian">sherlockian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1345943631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15627783421075258852">This one started out really strong for me, and then petered out near the end, for a couple of reasons, which I will be marking in spoiler tags.<br/><br/>Mary and Holmes continue to be a great pair, and I find their cerebral marriage a compelling one, though I am beginning to get a bit annoyed with King for discounting Watson so much. Here, we meet an old friend from the first book, the archaeologist Dorothy Ruskin, who shows up in Sussex for a visit, and leaves Mary and Holmes with a strange parchment, wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15627783421075258852" style="display:none">This one started out really strong for me, and then petered out near the end, for a couple of reasons, which I will be marking in spoiler tags.<br /><br />Mary and Holmes continue to be a great pair, and I find their cerebral marriage a compelling one, though I am beginning to get a bit annoyed with King for discounting Watson so much. Here, we meet an old friend from the first book, the archaeologist Dorothy Ruskin, who shows up in Sussex for a visit, and leaves Mary and Holmes with a strange parchment, which appears to have been written by Mary of Magdalene, and if verified, would strongly suggest she was one of Jesus's apostles. Soon after, Dorothy Ruskin is hit by a car and killed, and the Holmes cottage burglarized and turned upside down, the burglars obviously searching for something.<br /><br />The death of an eccentric old woman, and one that seems to be an accident, no less, is not high up on Scotland Yard's priority list, so Mary and Holmes head to London, roping Lestrade Jr. and Mycroft in to their informal investigation.<br /><br />I was enjoying this book quite a bit until about 2/3 of the way through, when it became clear that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Mary had been rather wasting her time in investigating Colonel Edwards, and Holmes (whose investigation of Dorothy Ruskin's sister mostly takes place "off screen") has the profitable end of the investigation, which we don't get to see. It was a puzzling choice, I thought, to have the Mary letter and Edwards both be red herrings, and to have Mary (our POV and protagonist) fixate on them both, only to have the solution lie elsewhere. <br /><br />In the author's notes, King mentions that it's not a pointless red herring, as through Edwards we see what kind of misogynistic response might lie ahead if she were to make the Magdalene letter public, but it left me feeling rather unsatisfied at the conclusion. Especially since Holmes doesn't really get his woman, either. There is a lack of evidence condemning the obviously guilty sister and her grandson, and Holmes, too, is left with little resolution, as they appear to have gotten away with it. This is partially mitigated by the end, when they discover another letter hidden in the box Dorothy Ruskin left them, but the motive being simple greed leaves everyone involved (including me) feeling sort of deflated. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Nevertheless, this was still an enjoyable, quick read, clocking in at only 275 pages. Mary's perspective on history, mixed with her interest in theology and detection, continues to make it worthwhile to read these books.<br /><br />[3.5 stars, rounded up]</span>
  <a data-text-id="15627783421075258852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2401151505" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2401151505" />
    <a title="Carlee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54039933-carlee"><img alt="Carlee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487289233p2/54039933.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2401151505?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carlee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carlee" href="/user/show/54039933-carlee">Carlee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54039933-carlee?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54039933-carlee?shelf=2018-r-e-a-d">2018-r-e-a-d</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2401151505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14557440650787232065">Another Mary Russell novel that I tackled as an audiobook. I loved the narrator the last time, Jenny Sterlin, and was happy to see her name as the narrator again.<br/><br/>This one has a lot more mystery involved and several layers to deduce, which made me love it more than the previous two. So, why the lower rating? Well, I have to often take a break from a series or I get burned out. While this was still a lovely book to listen to and far more interesting than the second book in the series, I did grow b</span>
  <span id="freeText14557440650787232065" style="display:none">Another Mary Russell novel that I tackled as an audiobook. I loved the narrator the last time, Jenny Sterlin, and was happy to see her name as the narrator again.<br /><br />This one has a lot more mystery involved and several layers to deduce, which made me love it more than the previous two. So, why the lower rating? Well, I have to often take a break from a series or I get burned out. While this was still a lovely book to listen to and far more interesting than the second book in the series, I did grow bored. I don’t think that is any fault to the book, though, just time for me to take a break from the series.<br /><br />Now, on to the nitty gritty.<br /><br />As I said, this was more interesting than the previous book. The first book was great, the second was okay and made better by the narrator, but this one was really interesting. Dorothy Ruskin, a female archaeologist (common now, but not so much in the setting of the novel), arrives on Holmes’ and Russell’s doorstep with an ancient piece of paper. On this paper, is what appears to be a letter written by none other than Mary Magdalene and if it is real, would change the interpretation of the Bible.<br /><br />If you’ve read any of the previous Mary Russell novels, which you should, you would know how important this document is to Mary. A letter than can change the way the Bible and subsequently all organized religions’ interpretations of it, would, essentially, change Mary. She has somehow managed to not only be of a scientific mind but also a religious one, which has always baffled Holmes, and the validation of this document is hard to resist. Dorothy Ruskin, though, ends up dead, the victim of a strange accident.<br /><br />Now, Mary and Sherlock have to go undercover to find out what happened to Dorothy and why.<br /><br />This was another great Sherlock-esque novel. There were a handful of players with their hands in the pot and different layers to work through and put the puzzle pieces together. It had a lot more to put together compared to the previous two novels in the series, which I liked. There were also more instances of everything almost falling apart. Mary has to go undercover and she comes close to getting figured out, which creates a wonderful level of suspense.<br /><br />The aspect that I loved from the very beginning, the banter, was back in this one. There is something about well-done dialogue that really brings a story to life and this one had that well-known Sherlock dialogue. It was a great story to listen to and I do look forward to continuing the series, I just need to take a small break to not get burned out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14557440650787232065" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8329716060255777626">"A Letter of Mary" is a papyrus scrap, penned by a Mariam, apostle of Jesus, to her sister Judith of Magdala, commending the carrier Rachel, her grand-daughter to their care, days before the fall of Israel to the Roman heathens. Days after Dorothy Ruskin presents the gift in a delicate dainty wooden box to friends Mary and Sherlock Holmes, a car runs down and kills the white-haired intrepid archaeologist. Evidence of a deliberate trip-wire points to murder. Men with black hair and sharp knives v</span>
  <span id="freeText8329716060255777626" style="display:none">"A Letter of Mary" is a papyrus scrap, penned by a Mariam, apostle of Jesus, to her sister Judith of Magdala, commending the carrier Rachel, her grand-daughter to their care, days before the fall of Israel to the Roman heathens. Days after Dorothy Ruskin presents the gift in a delicate dainty wooden box to friends Mary and Sherlock Holmes, a car runs down and kills the white-haired intrepid archaeologist. Evidence of a deliberate trip-wire points to murder. Men with black hair and sharp knives vandalize and steal from the Holmes cottage. <br /><br />Holmes goes undercover as an itinerant worker for Mrs. Erica Rogers, angry sister. Mary is a meek secretary to misogynist Colonel Dennis Edwards, last person to see her alive, both angry. Lestrade follows up on witnesses and alibis, such as dark-haired male relatives. Mycroft investigates her Zionist work having angered previously friendly dark-haired Palestinians, and hosts weekly team evenings. They dine together, then exchange leads and clues. The tedious summary reports remind me of annoying typewritten repetitions in #11, so I hope the feature does not continue.<br /><br />The Watson version, wherein we skip past dead-ends and tedious waiting, is more to my liking. I never like dreams, hypnosis, or other flim-flam that brings down otherwise realistic fiction. Mentioning Peter (Wimsey according to other references) and Tolkein, is flash, like show-off of author's scholarly minutiae. <br /><br />But I'm hooked on Holmes, his clever determined side-kick Russell, and the flavor of London fog and Sussex downs. With few pages left, we fear all is lost, the criminals will escape punishment again, King pull out a surprise, and wins again. Sequels await on my shelf.<br /><br />I understand neither the preface, nor the greek letters, nor verses preceding chapters. 4* for the essential story. 3* for the slowing down parts. Once we know the villains, where is the suspense?<br />Typo: p200 neither incident nor (not not) illegality</span>
  <a data-text-id="8329716060255777626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_273827052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273827052" />
    <a title="Christy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1068292-christy"><img alt="Christy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230744305p2/1068292.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273827052?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christy" href="/user/show/1068292-christy">Christy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273827052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6511761457159459923">This is the third Sherlock Holmes/Mary Russell mystery, and I read it, hoping to find the magic that captivated me in “The Beekeeper’s Apprentice.” It isn’t here. The plot is poorly constructed, mainly focusing on a wild goose chase concerning a character that might possibly be the killer. Then, the real killer pops in out of the blue, and we realize that we were completely suckered, having wasted our time with 200 pages of nothing that mattered at all! And then there’s the title, which refers t</span>
  <span id="freeText6511761457159459923" style="display:none">This is the third Sherlock Holmes/Mary Russell mystery, and I read it, hoping to find the magic that captivated me in “The Beekeeper’s Apprentice.” It isn’t here. The plot is poorly constructed, mainly focusing on a wild goose chase concerning a character that might possibly be the killer. Then, the real killer pops in out of the blue, and we realize that we were completely suckered, having wasted our time with 200 pages of nothing that mattered at all! And then there’s the title, which refers to a letter supposedly written by Mary Magdalene, proving she was an apostle of Jesus. Does this letter have anything to do with the plot? Not at all! I’m sorry to see this series fizzle out, because Laurie King is a very talented writer. But that’s not enough, without coherent plot lines. By the way, I heard book #4 (“The Moor”) wasn’t very good, so I skipped ahead to Book #5, “O Jerusalem.” It was so boring that I quit after 50 pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6511761457159459923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
